   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Siphon
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about the device. For the carbonation tool, see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_siphon" title="Soda siphon">
        soda siphon
       </a>
       . For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Siphon_(disambiguation)" title="Siphon (disambiguation)">
        Siphon (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lappo.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="335" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lappo.svg/220px-Lappo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lappo.svg/330px-Lappo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lappo.svg/440px-Lappo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lappo.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Siphon principle
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FlyingDropletSiphon.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="936" data-file-width="680" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/FlyingDropletSiphon.svg/220px-FlyingDropletSiphon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/FlyingDropletSiphon.svg/330px-FlyingDropletSiphon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/FlyingDropletSiphon.svg/440px-FlyingDropletSiphon.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FlyingDropletSiphon.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 2 - In the flying droplet siphon, surface tension pulls the stream of liquid into separate droplets inside of a sealed, air filled chamber, preventing the liquid going down from having contact with the liquid going up, and thereby preventing liquid tensile strength from pulling the liquid up. It also demonstrates that the effect of atmospheric pressure at the entrance is not canceled by the equal atmospheric pressure at the exit.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The word
       <b>
        siphon
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">
            aɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'f' in 'find'">
            f
           </span>
           <span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">
            ən
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          SY
         </span>
         -fən
        </i>
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">
        Ancient Greek
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        σίφων
       </span>
       "pipe, tube", also spelled
       <b>
        syphon
       </b>
       ) is used to refer to a wide variety of devices that involve the flow of liquids through tubes. In a narrower sense, the word refers particularly to a tube in an inverted 'U' shape, which causes a liquid to flow upward, above the surface of a reservoir, with no pump, but powered by the fall of the liquid as it flows down the tube under the pull of
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       , then discharging at a level lower than the surface of the reservoir from which it came.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are two leading theories about how siphons cause liquid to flow uphill, against gravity, without being pumped, and powered only by gravity. The traditional theory for centuries was that gravity pulling the liquid down on the exit side of the siphon resulted in reduced pressure at the top of the siphon. Then atmospheric pressure was able to push the liquid from the upper reservoir, up into the reduced pressure at the top of the siphon, like in a barometer or drinking straw, and then over.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramette_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ramette-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richert_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Richert-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuire_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McGuire-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor1914_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Minor1914-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it has been demonstrated that siphons can operate in a vacuum
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor1914_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Minor1914-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boatwright2015_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Boatwright2015-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duane1902_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Duane1902-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and to heights exceeding the barometric height of the liquid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor1914_5-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Minor1914-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boatwright2015_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Boatwright2015-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndrewKFletcher_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AndrewKFletcher-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Consequently, the cohesion tension theory of siphon operation has been advocated, where the liquid is pulled over the siphon in a way similar to the chain model.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeadChainSiphon_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeadChainSiphon-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It need not be one theory or the other that is correct, but rather both theories may be correct in different circumstances of ambient pressure. The atmospheric pressure with gravity theory obviously cannot explain siphons in vacuum, where there is no significant atmospheric pressure. But the cohesion tension with gravity theory cannot explain CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       gas siphons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CO2_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CO2-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       siphons working despite bubbles, and the flying droplet siphon, where gases do not exert significant pulling forces, and liquids not in contact cannot exert a cohesive tension force.
      </p>
      <p>
       All known published theories in modern times recognize
       <a href="#Explanation_using_Bernoulli.27s_equation">
        Bernoulli's equation
       </a>
       as a decent approximation to idealized, friction-free siphon operation.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Explanation using Bernoulli's equation
          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        Egyptian
       </a>
       reliefs from 1500 BC depict siphons used to extract liquids from large storage jars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com.au_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.com.au-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is physical evidence for the use of siphons by
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        Greek
       </a>
       engineers in the 3rd century BC at
       <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">
        Pergamon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com.au_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.com.au-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">
        Hero of Alexandria
       </a>
       wrote extensively about siphons in the treatise
       <i>
        Pneumatica
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Musa" title="Banu Musa">
        Banu Musa
       </a>
       brothers of 9th-century Baghdad invented a double-concentric siphon, which they described in their
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ingenious_Devices" title="Book of Ingenious Devices">
         Book of Ingenious Devices
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill-21_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hill-21-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The edition edited by Hill includes an analysis of the double-concentric siphon.
      </p>
      <p>
       Siphons were studied further in the 17th century, in the context of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suction_pump" title="Suction pump">
        suction pumps
       </a>
       (and the recently developed
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">
        vacuum pumps
       </a>
       ), particularly with an eye to understanding the
       <a href="#Maximum_height">
        maximum height
       </a>
       of pumps (and siphons) and the apparent vacuum at the top of early
       <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">
        barometers
       </a>
       . This was initially explained by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       via the theory of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Horror_vacui_(physics)" title="Horror vacui (physics)">
         horror vacui
        </a>
       </i>
       ("nature abhors a vacuum"), which dates to
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       , and which Galileo restated as
       <i>
        resintenza del vacuo,
       </i>
       but this was subsequently disproved by later workers, notably
       <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">
        Evangelista Torricelli
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">
        Blaise Pascal
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       – see
       <a href="/wiki/Barometer#History" title="Barometer">
        barometer: history
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">
        Theory
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Theory">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChainModelOfSiphon150Pixels.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="168" data-file-width="150" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/ChainModelOfSiphon150Pixels.jpg" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChainModelOfSiphon150Pixels.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The chain model – where the section marked "B" pulls down because it is heavier than the section "A" – is a flawed but useful analogy to the operation of a siphon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A practical siphon, operating at typical atmospheric pressures and tube heights, works because gravity pulling down on the taller column of liquid leaves reduced pressure at the top of the siphon (formally,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_pressure" title="Hydrostatic pressure">
        hydrostatic pressure
       </a>
       when the liquid is not moving). This reduced pressure at the top means gravity pulling down on the shorter column of liquid is not sufficient to keep the liquid stationary against the atmospheric pressure pushing it up into the reduced pressure zone at the top of the siphon. So the liquid flows from the higher pressure area of the upper reservoir, up to the lower pressure zone at the top of the siphon, over the top, and then with the help of gravity and a taller column of liquid, down to the higher pressure zone at the exit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichertHawaii_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RichertHawaii-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micolich_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-micolich-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The chain model is a useful but not completely accurate conceptual model of a siphon. The chain model helps to understand how a siphon can cause liquid to flow uphill, powered only by the downward force of gravity. A siphon can sometimes be thought of a little like a chain hanging over a pulley, with one end of the chain piled on a higher surface than the other. Since the length of chain on the shorter side is lighter than the length of chain on the taller side, the heavier chain on the taller side will move down and pull up the chain on the lighter side. Similar to a siphon, the chain model is obviously just powered by gravity acting on the heavier side, and there is clearly no violation of conservation of energy, because the chain is ultimately just moving from a higher to a lower location, as the liquid does in a siphon.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="175" data-file-width="125" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg/220px-SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg/330px-SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/68/SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg/440px-SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiphonStillWorksWithBigLeg.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Even the falling lighter lower leg from C to D can cause the liquid of the heavier upper leg to flow up and over into the lower reservoir
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planinsic_21-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Planinsic-21">
           [21]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are a number of problems with the chain model of a siphon, and understanding these differences helps to explain the actual workings of siphons. First, unlike in the chain model of the siphon, it is not actually the
       <i>
        weight
       </i>
       on the taller side compared to the shorter side, that matters. Rather it is the difference in
       <i>
        height
       </i>
       from the reservoir surfaces to the top of the siphon, that determines the balance of pressure. For example, if the tube from the upper reservoir to the top of the siphon has a much larger diameter than the taller section of tube from the lower reservoir to the top of the siphon, the shorter upper section of the siphon may have a much larger weight of liquid in it, and yet the lighter volume of liquid in the down tube can pull liquid up the fatter up tube, and the siphon can function normally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planinsic_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Planinsic-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another difference is that under most practical circumstances, dissolved gases, vapor pressure, and (sometimes) lack of adhesion with tube walls, conspire to render the tensile strength within the liquid ineffective for siphoning. Thus, unlike a chain which has significant tensile strength, liquids usually have little tensile strength under typical siphon conditions, and therefore the liquid on the rising side cannot be pulled up, in the way the chain is pulled up on the rising side.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micolich_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-micolich-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="133" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d4/SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg/220px-SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d4/SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg/330px-SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d4/SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg/440px-SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiphonNoTensileStrengthNeeded.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 5 - Air Start Siphon - When the column of liquid is allowed to fall from C down to D, liquid in the upper reservoir will flow up to B and over the top.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichertHawaii_19-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-RichertHawaii-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micolich_20-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-micolich-20">
           [20]
          </a>
         </sup>
         No liquid tensile strength is needed to pull the liquid up.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/640px--AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="5.5858503401361" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="AirLaunchSiphon.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 640 × 480 (702 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="640" data-height="480" data-bandwidth="702435" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="SD WebM (480P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 480P" data-transcodekey="480p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="480" data-bandwidth="977144" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="133944" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="253160" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="480" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="488400" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:AirLaunchSiphon.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv/220px-seek%3D0-AirLaunchSiphon.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4b/AirLaunchSiphon.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AirLaunchSiphon.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Demonstration of siphoning tropical fruit punch with a flying droplet siphon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       An occasional misunderstanding of siphons is that they rely on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">
        tensile strength
       </a>
       of the liquid to pull the liquid up and over the rise.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichertHawaii_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-RichertHawaii-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micolich_20-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-micolich-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While water has been found to have a great deal of tensile strength in some experiments (such as with the
       <a href="/wiki/Z-tube" title="Z-tube">
        z-tube
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), and siphons in vacuum rely on such cohesion, common siphons can easily be demonstrated to need no liquid tensile strength at all to function.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichertHawaii_19-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-RichertHawaii-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micolich_20-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-micolich-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, since common siphons operate at positive pressures throughout the siphon, there is no contribution from liquid tensile strength, because the molecules are actually repelling each other in order to resist the pressure, rather than pulling on each other.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To demonstrate, the longer lower leg of a common siphon can be plugged at the bottom and filled almost to the crest with liquid as in Figure 5, leaving the top and the shorter upper leg completely dry and containing only air. When the plug is removed and the liquid in the longer lower leg is allowed to fall, the liquid in the upper reservoir will then typically sweep the air bubble down and out of the tube. The apparatus will then continue to operate as a normal siphon. As there is no contact between the liquid on either side of the siphon at the beginning of this experiment, there can be no cohesion between the liquid molecules to pull the liquid over the rise. It has been suggested by advocates of the liquid tensile strength theory, that the air start siphon only demonstrates the effect as the siphon starts, but that the situation changes after the bubble is swept out and the siphon achieves steady flow. But a similar effect can be seen in the flying droplet siphon of figure 2. The flying droplet siphon works continuously without liquid tensile strength pulling the liquid up. The siphon in the video demonstration operated steadily for more than 28 minutes until the upper reservoir was empty. Another simple demonstration that liquid tensile strength isn't needed in the siphon is to simply introduce a bubble into the siphon during operation. The bubble can be large enough to entirely disconnect the liquids in the tube before and after the bubble, defeating any liquid tensile strength, and yet if the bubble isn't too big, the siphon will continue to operate with little change as it sweeps the bubble out.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another common misconception about siphons, is that because the atmospheric pressure is virtually identical at the entrance and exit, the atmospheric pressure cancels, and therefore atmospheric pressure can't be pushing the liquid up the siphon. But equal and opposite forces may not completely cancel if there is an intervening force that counters some or all of one of the forces. In the siphon, the atmospheric pressure at the entrance and exit are both lessened by the force of gravity pulling down the liquid in each tube, but the pressure on the down side is lessened more by the taller column of liquid on the down side. In effect, the atmospheric pressure coming up the down side doesn't entirely "make it" to the top to cancel all of the atmospheric pressure pushing up the up side. This effect can be seen more easily in the example of two carts being pushed up opposite sides of a hill. As shown in the diagram, even though the person on the left seems to have his push canceled entirely by the equal and opposite push from the person on the right, the person on the left's seemingly canceled push is still the source of the force to push the left cart up.
      </p>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="1054" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg/480px-OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg/720px-OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg/960px-OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg.png 2x" width="480"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OppositeAndEqualForcesNotCancelling.svg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          An example of equal and opposite forces that would seem to cancel each other, yet the seemingly cancelled force from the left, still pushes the object up, similar to how the equal and opposite atmospheric pressure at each end of a siphon, that would seem to cancel, leaves atmospheric pressure still able to push the liquid up.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In some situations siphons do function in the absence of atmospheric pressure and via tensile strength – see
       <a href="#Vacuum_siphons">
        vacuum siphons
       </a>
       – and in these situations the chain model can be instructive. Further, in other settings water transport does occur via tension, most significantly in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transpirational_pull" title="Transpirational pull">
        transpirational pull
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Xylem" title="Xylem">
        xylem
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">
        vascular plants
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichertHawaii_19-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-RichertHawaii-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hughes-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Water and other liquids may seem to have no tensile strength because when a handful is scooped up and pulled on, the liquids narrow and pull apart effortlessly. But liquid tensile strength in a siphon is possible when the liquid adheres to the tube walls and thereby resists narrowing. Any contamination on the tube walls, such as grease or air bubbles, or other minor influences such as turbulence or vibration, can cause the liquid to detach from the walls and lose all tensile strength.
      </p>
      <p>
       In more detail, one can look at how the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_pressure" title="Hydrostatic pressure">
        hydrostatic pressure
       </a>
       varies through a static siphon, considering in turn the vertical tube from the top reservoir, the vertical tube from the bottom reservoir, and the horizontal tube connecting them (assuming a U-shape). At liquid level in the top reservoir, the liquid is under atmospheric pressure, and as one goes up the siphon, the hydrostatic pressure decreases (under
       <a href="/wiki/Vertical_pressure_variation" title="Vertical pressure variation">
        vertical pressure variation
       </a>
       ), since the weight of atmospheric pressure pushing the water up is counterbalanced by the column of water in the siphon pushing down (until one reaches the maximum height of a barometer/siphon, at which point the liquid cannot be pushed higher) – the hydrostatic pressure at the top of the tube is then lower than atmospheric pressure by an amount proportional to the height of the tube. Doing the same analysis on the tube rising from the lower reservoir yields the pressure at the top of that (vertical) tube; this pressure is lower because the tube is longer (there is more water pushing down), and requires that the lower reservoir is lower than the upper reservoir, or more generally that the discharge outlet simply be lower than the surface of the upper reservoir. Considering now the horizontal tube connecting them, one sees that the pressure at the top of the tube from the top reservoir is higher (since less water is being lifted), while the pressure at the top of the tube from the bottom reservoir is lower (since more water is being lifted), and since liquids move from high pressure to low pressure, the liquid flows across the horizontal tube from the top basin to the bottom basin. Note that the liquid is under positive pressure (compression) throughout the tube, not tension.
      </p>
      <p>
       Bernoulli's equation is considered in the scientific literature to be a fair approximation to the operation of the siphon. In non-ideal fluids, compressibility, tensile strength and other characteristics of the working fluid (or multiple fluids) complicate Bernoulli's equation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Once started, a siphon requires no additional
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       to keep the liquid flowing up and out of the reservoir. The siphon will draw liquid out of the reservoir until the level falls below the intake, allowing air or other surrounding gas to break the siphon, or until the outlet of the siphon equals the level of the reservoir, whichever comes first.
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">
        atmospheric pressure
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       of the liquid, and
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">
        gravity
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="#Maximum_height">
        maximum height
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Crest_(physics)" title="Crest (physics)">
        crest
       </a>
       in practical siphons is limited by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapour_pressure" title="Vapour pressure">
        vapour pressure
       </a>
       of the liquid. When the pressure within the liquid drops to below the liquid's vapor pressure, tiny vapor bubbles can begin to form at the high point and the siphon effect will end. This effect depends on how efficiently the liquid can
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleate" title="Nucleate">
        nucleate
       </a>
       bubbles; in the absence of impurities or rough surfaces to act as easy nucleation sites for bubbles, siphons can temporarily exceed their standard maximum height during the extended time it takes bubbles to nucleate. One siphon of degassed water was demonstrated to 24 meters for an extended period of time
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndrewKFletcher_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AndrewKFletcher-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and other controlled experiments to 10 meters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_atmospheric_pressure" title="Standard atmospheric pressure">
        standard atmospheric pressure
       </a>
       , the maximum siphon height is approximately 10
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        m
       </a>
       (32
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foot_(length)" title="Foot (length)">
        feet
       </a>
       ); for
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">
        mercury
       </a>
       it is 76 cm (30
       <a href="/wiki/Inch" title="Inch">
        inches
       </a>
       ), which is the definition of standard pressure. This equals the maximum height of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suction_pump" title="Suction pump">
        suction pump
       </a>
       , which operates by the same principle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ratio of heights (about 13.6) equals the ratio of densities of water and mercury (at a given temperature), since the column of water (resp. mercury) is balancing with the column of air yielding atmospheric pressure, and indeed maximum height is (neglecting vapor pressure and velocity of liquid) inversely proportional to density of liquid.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_research_into_the_operation_of_the_siphon">
        Modern research into the operation of the siphon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern research into the operation of the siphon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In 1948,
       <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Nokes" title="Malcolm Nokes">
        Malcolm Nokes
       </a>
       investigated siphons working in both
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pressure" title="Air pressure">
        air pressure
       </a>
       and in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_vacuum" title="Partial vacuum">
        partial vacuum
       </a>
       , for siphons in vacuum he concluded that: "The gravitational force on the column of liquid in the downtake tube less the gravitational force in the uptake tube causes the liquid to move. The liquid is therefore in tension and sustains a longitudinal strain which, in the absence of disturbing factors, is insufficient to break the column of liquid". But for siphons of small uptake height working at atmospheric pressure, he concluded that: "... the tension of the liquid column is neutralized and
       <i>
        reversed
       </i>
       by the compressive effect of the atmosphere on the opposite ends of the liquid column."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Potter and Barnes at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">
        University of Edinburgh
       </a>
       revisited siphons in 1971. They re-examined the theories of the siphon and ran experiments on siphons in air pressure. Their conclusion was that; "By now it should be clear that, despite a wealth of tradition, the basic mechanism of a siphon does not depend upon atmospheric pressure."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        Gravity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">
        pressure
       </a>
       and molecular
       <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(chemistry)" title="Cohesion (chemistry)">
        cohesion
       </a>
       were the focus of work in 2010 by Hughes at the
       <a href="/wiki/Queensland_University_of_Technology" title="Queensland University of Technology">
        Queensland University of Technology
       </a>
       . He used siphons at air pressure and his conclusion was that: "The flow of water out of the bottom of a siphon depends on the difference in height between the inflow and outflow, and therefore cannot be dependent on atmospheric pressure…"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hughes did further work on siphons at air pressure in 2011 and concluded that: "The experiments described above demonstrate that ordinary siphons at atmospheric pressure operate through gravity and not atmospheric pressure".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The father and son researchers, Ramette and Ramette, successfully siphoned
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       under air pressure in 2011 and concluded that molecular cohesion is not required for the operation of a siphon but that: "The basic explanation of siphon action is that, once the tube is filled, the flow is initiated by the greater pull of gravity on the fluid on the longer side compared with that on the short side. This creates a pressure drop throughout the siphon tube, in the same sense that 'sucking' on a straw reduces the pressure along its length all the way to the intake point. The ambient atmospheric pressure at the intake point responds to the reduced pressure by forcing the fluid upwards, sustaining the flow, just as in a steadily sucked straw in a milkshake."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramette_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ramette-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Again in 2011, Richert and Binder (at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">
        University of Hawaii
       </a>
       ) examined the siphon and concluded that molecular cohesion is not required for the operation of a siphon but relies upon gravity and a pressure differential, writing: "As the fluid initially primed on the long leg of the siphon rushes down due to gravity, it leaves behind a partial vacuum that allows pressure on the entrance point of the higher container to push fluid up the leg on that side".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richert_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Richert-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The research team of Boatwright, Puttick, and Licence, all at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">
        University of Nottingham
       </a>
       , succeeded in running a siphon in
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
        high vacuum
       </a>
       , also in 2011. They wrote that: "It is widely believed that the siphon is principally driven by the force of atmospheric pressure. An experiment is described that shows that a siphon can function even under high-vacuum conditions. Molecular cohesion and gravity are shown to be contributing factors in the operation of a siphon; the presence of a positive atmospheric pressure is not required".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Writing in
       <a href="/wiki/Physics_Today" title="Physics Today">
        Physics Today
       </a>
       in 2011, J. Dooley from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millersville_University" title="Millersville University">
        Millersville University
       </a>
       stated that both a pressure differential within the siphon tube and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">
        tensile strength
       </a>
       of the liquid are required for a siphon to operate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A researcher at
       <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_State_University" title="Humboldt State University">
        Humboldt State University
       </a>
       , A. McGuire, examined flow in siphons in 2012. Using the advanced general-purpose multiphysics simulation software package
       <a href="/wiki/LS-DYNA" title="LS-DYNA">
        LS-DYNA
       </a>
       he examined pressure initialisation, flow, and pressure propagation within a siphon. He concluded that: "Pressure, gravity and molecular cohesion can all be driving forces in the operation of siphons".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuire_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McGuire-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2014, Hughes and Gurung (at the Queensland University of Technology), ran a water siphon under varying air pressures ranging from sea level to 11.9 km (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7004118872000000000♠
        </span>
        39
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        ft
       </span>
       ) altitude. They noted that: "Flow remained more or less constant during ascension indicating that siphon flow is independent of ambient
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barometric_pressure" title="Barometric pressure">
        barometric pressure
       </a>
       ". They used
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_equation" title="Bernoulli's equation">
        Bernoulli's equation
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poiseuille_equation" title="Poiseuille equation">
        Poiseuille equation
       </a>
       to examine pressure differentials and fluid flow within a siphon. Their conclusion was that: "It follows from the above analysis that there must be a direct cohesive connection between water molecules flowing in and out of a siphon. This is true at all atmospheric pressures in which the pressure in the apex of the siphon is above the vapour pressure of water, an exception being ionic liquids".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_requirements">
        Practical requirements
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Practical requirements">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A plain tube can be used as a siphon. An external
       <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">
        pump
       </a>
       has to be applied to start the liquid flowing and
       <i>
        prime
       </i>
       the siphon. This can be a human mouth. This is sometimes done with any leak-free hose to siphon
       <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
        gasoline
       </a>
       from a motor vehicle's gasoline tank to an external tank. (Siphoning gasoline by mouth often results in the accidental swallowing of gasoline, or
       <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_aspiration" title="Pulmonary aspiration">
        aspirating
       </a>
       it into the lungs, which can cause death or lung damage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) If the tube is flooded with liquid before part of the tube is raised over the intermediate high point and care is taken to keep the tube flooded while it is being raised, no pump is required. Devices sold as siphons often come with a
       <i>
        siphon
        <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">
         pump
        </a>
       </i>
       to start the siphon process.
      </p>
      <p>
       In some applications it can be helpful to use siphon tubing that is not much larger than necessary. Using piping of too great a diameter and then throttling the flow using valves or constrictive piping appears to increase the effect of previously cited concerns over gases or vapor collecting in the crest which serve to break the vacuum. If the vacuum is reduced too much, the siphon effect can be lost. Reducing the size of pipe used closer to requirements appears to reduce this effect and creates a more functional siphon that does not require constant re-priming and restarting. In this respect, where the requirement is to match a flow into a container with a flow out of said container (to maintain a constant level in a pond fed by a stream, for example) it would be preferable to utilize two or three smaller separate parallel pipes that can be started as required rather than attempting to use a single large pipe and attempting to throttle it.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Automatic_intermittent_siphon">
        Automatic intermittent siphon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Automatic intermittent siphon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Siphons are sometimes employed as automatic machines, in situations where it is desirable to turn a continuous trickling flow or an irregular small surge flow into a large surge volume. A common example of this is a public restroom with urinals regularly flushed by an automatic siphon in a small water tank overhead. When the container is filled, all the stored liquid is released, emerging as a large surge volume that then resets and fills again. One way to do this intermittent action involves complex machinery such as floats, chains, levers, and valves, but these can corrode, wear out, or jam over time. An alternate method is with rigid pipes and chambers, using only the water itself in a siphon as the operating mechanism.
      </p>
      <p>
       A siphon used in an automatic unattended device needs to be able to function reliably without failure. This is different from the common demonstration self-starting siphons in that there are ways the siphon can fail to function which require manual intervention to return to normal surge flow operation.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most common failure is for the liquid to dribble out slowly, matching the rate that the container is filling, and the siphon enters an undesired steady-state condition. Preventing dribbling typically involves pneumatic principles to trap one or more large air bubbles in various pipes, which are sealed by water traps. This method can fail if it can't start working intermittently without water already present in parts of the mechanism, and which will not be filled if the mechanism starts from a dry state.
      </p>
      <p>
       A second problem is that the trapped air pockets will shrink over time if the siphon is not operating due to no inflow. The air in pockets is absorbed by the liquid, which pulls liquid up into the piping until the air pocket disappears, and can cause activation of water flow outside the normal range of operating when the storage tank is not full, leading to loss of the liquid seal in lower parts of the mechanism.
      </p>
      <p>
       A third problem is where the lower end of the liquid seal is simply a U-trap bend in an outflow pipe. During vigorous emptying, the kinetic motion of the liquid out the outflow can propel too much liquid out, causing a loss of the sealing volume in the outflow trap and loss of the trapped air bubble to maintain intermittent operation.
      </p>
      <p>
       A fourth problem involves seep holes in the mechanism, intended to slowly refill these various sealing chambers when the siphon is dry. The seep holes can be plugged by debris and corrosion, requiring manual cleaning and intervention. To prevent this, the siphon may be restricted to pure liquid sources, free of solids or precipitate.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many automatic siphons have been invented going back to at least the 1850s, for automatic siphon mechanisms that attempt to overcome these problems using various pneumatic and hydrodynamic principles.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications_and_terminology">
        Applications and terminology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Applications and terminology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siphon.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1924" data-file-width="2028" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Siphon.JPG/220px-Siphon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Siphon.JPG/330px-Siphon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Siphon.JPG/440px-Siphon.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siphon.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A siphon used for
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homebrewing_beer" title="Homebrewing beer">
          homebrewing beer
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siphoning.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2490" data-file-width="1562" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Siphoning.JPG/220px-Siphoning.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Siphoning.JPG/330px-Siphoning.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Siphoning.JPG/440px-Siphoning.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siphoning.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Siphoning the beer after a first
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beer_fermentation" title="Beer fermentation">
          fermentation
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When certain liquids needs to be purified, siphoning can help prevent either the bottom (
       <a href="/wiki/Sediment#Dregs" title="Sediment">
        dregs
       </a>
       ) or the top (
       <a href="/wiki/Foam" title="Foam">
        foam
       </a>
       and floaties) from being transferred out of one container into a new container. Siphoning is thus useful in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">
        fermentation
       </a>
       of wine and beer for this reason, since it can keep unwanted impurities out of the new container.
      </p>
      <p>
       Self-constructed siphons, made of pipes or tubes, can be used to evacuate water from cellars after floodings. Between the flooded cellar and a deeper place outside a connection is built, using a tube or some pipes. They are filled with water through an intake valve (at the highest end of the construction). When the ends are opened, the water flows through the pipe into the sewer or the river.
      </p>
      <p>
       Siphoning is common in irrigated fields to transfer a controlled amount of water from a ditch, over the ditch wall, into furrows.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg/220px-StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg/330px-StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg/440px-StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StGeorgeCottonIrrig.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Siphon irrigation of cotton at
         <a href="/wiki/St_George,_Queensland" title="St George, Queensland">
          St George, Queensland
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Large siphons may be used in municipal
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterworks" title="Waterworks">
        waterworks
       </a>
       and industry. Their size requires control via valves at the intake, outlet and crest of the siphon. The siphon may be primed by closing the intake and outlets and filling the siphon at the crest. If intakes and outlets are submerged, a
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">
        vacuum pump
       </a>
       may be applied at the crest to prime the siphon. Alternatively the siphon may be primed by a pump at either the intake or outlet.
      </p>
      <p>
       Gas in the liquid is a concern in large siphons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The gas tends to accumulate at the crest and if enough accumulates to break the flow of liquid, the siphon stops working. The siphon itself will exacerbate the problem because as the liquid is raised through the siphon, the pressure drops, causing dissolved gases within the liquid to come out of solution. Higher temperature accelerates the release of gas from liquids so maintaining a constant, low temperature helps. The longer the liquid is in the siphon, the more gas is released, so a shorter siphon overall helps. Local high points will trap gas so the intake and outlet legs should have continuous slopes without intermediate high points. The flow of the liquid moves bubbles thus the intake leg can have a shallow slope as the flow will push the gas bubbles to the crest. Conversely, the outlet leg needs to have a steep slope to allow the bubbles to move against the liquid flow; though other designs call for a shallow slope in the outlet leg as well to allow the bubbles to be carried out of the siphon. At the crest the gas can be trapped in a chamber above the crest. The chamber needs to be occasionally primed again with liquid to remove the gas.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_rain_gauge">
        Siphon rain gauge
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Siphon rain gauge">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        siphon rain gauge
       </i>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">
        rain gauge
       </a>
       that can record rainfall over an extended period. A siphon is used to automatically empty the gauge. It is often simply called a "siphon gauge" and is not to be confused with a siphon pressure gauge.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_spillway">
        Siphon spillway
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Siphon spillway">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A siphon
       <a href="/wiki/Spillway" title="Spillway">
        spillway
       </a>
       in a dam is usually not technically a siphon as it is generally used to drain elevated water levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, a siphon spillway operates as an actual siphon if it raises the flow higher than the surface of the source reservoir, as sometimes is the case when used in irrigation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In operation, a siphon spillway is considered to be 'pipe flow' or 'closed duct flow'.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A normal spillway flow is pressurized by the height of the reservoir above the spillway whereas a siphon flow rate is governed by the difference in height of the inlet and outlet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some designs make use of an automatic system that uses the flow of water in a spiral vortex to remove the air above to prime the siphon. Such a design includes the volute siphon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Flush_Toilet">
        Flush Toilet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flush Toilet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Flush_toilet" title="Flush toilet">
        Flush toilets
       </a>
       often have some siphon effect as the bowl empties.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some toilets also use the siphon principle to obtain the actual flush from the
       <a href="/wiki/Cistern" title="Cistern">
        cistern
       </a>
       . The flush is triggered by a lever or handle that operates a simple diaphragm-like piston pump that lifts enough water to the crest of the siphon to start the flow of water which then completely empties the contents of the cistern into the toilet bowl. The advantage of this system was that no water would leak from the cistern excepting when flushed. These were mandatory in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">
        UK
       </a>
       until 2011.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Early
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinal_(restroom)" title="Urinal (restroom)">
        urinals
       </a>
       incorporated a siphon in the cistern which would flush automatically on a regular cycle because there was a constant trickle of clean water being fed to the cistern by a slightly open valve.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Devices_that_are_not_true_siphons">
        Devices that are not true siphons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Devices that are not true siphons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_coffee">
        Siphon coffee
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Siphon coffee">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siphon_coffee" title="Siphon coffee">
        Siphon coffee
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vakumpot_north.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1000" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vakumpot_north.svg/220px-Vakumpot_north.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vakumpot_north.svg/330px-Vakumpot_north.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vakumpot_north.svg/440px-Vakumpot_north.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vakumpot_north.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Siphon coffee brewer: when warmed by a heat source (A), vapor pressure increases in the lower chamber (B), forcing the water downwards (C) and through the central pipe into the upper chamber (D) where it is mixed with the coffee grounds. When the heat is removed, the water flows back down.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While if both ends of a siphon are at atmospheric pressure, liquid flows from high to low, if the bottom end of a siphon is pressurized, liquid can flow from low to high. If pressure is removed from the bottom end, the liquid flow will reverse, illustrating that it is pressure driving the siphon. An everyday illustration of this is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siphon_coffee" title="Siphon coffee">
        siphon coffee
       </a>
       brewer, which works as follows (designs vary; this is a standard design, omitting coffee grounds):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        a glass vessel is filled with water, then corked (so air-tight) with a siphon sticking vertically upwards
       </li>
       <li>
        another glass vessel is placed on top, open to the atmosphere – the top vessel is empty, the bottom is filled with water
       </li>
       <li>
        the bottom vessel is then heated; as the temperature increases, the
        <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">
         vapor pressure
        </a>
        of the water increases (it increasingly evaporates); when the water boils the vapor pressure equals atmospheric pressure, and as the temperature increases above boiling the pressure in the bottom vessel then
        <i>
         exceeds
        </i>
        atmospheric pressure, and pushes the water up the siphon tube into the upper vessel.
       </li>
       <li>
        a small amount of still hot water and steam remain in the bottom vessel and are kept heated, with this pressure keeping the water in the upper vessel
       </li>
       <li>
        when the heat is removed from the bottom vessel, the vapor pressure decreases, and can no longer support the column of water – gravity (acting on the water) and atmospheric pressure then push the water back into the bottom vessel.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In practice, the top vessel is filled with coffee grounds, and the heat is removed from the bottom vessel when the coffee has finished brewing. What vapor pressure means concretely is that the boiling water converts high-density water (a liquid) into low-density steam (a gas), which thus expands to take up more volume (in other words, the pressure increases). This pressure from the expanding steam then forces the liquid up the siphon; when the steam then condenses down to water the pressure decreases and the liquid flows back down.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_pump">
        Siphon pump
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Siphon pump">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       While a simple siphon cannot output liquid at a level higher than the source reservoir, a more complicated device utilizing an airtight metering chamber at the crest and a system of automatic valves, may discharge liquid on an ongoing basis, at a level higher than the source reservoir, without outside pumping energy being added. It can accomplish this despite what initially appears to be a violation of conservation of energy because it can take advantage of the energy of a large volume of liquid dropping some distance, to raise and discharge a small volume of liquid above the source reservoir. Thus it might be said to "require" a large quantity of falling liquid to power the dispensing of a small quantity. Such a system typically operates in a cyclical or start/stop but ongoing and self-powered manner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiphonPumpPatent_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SiphonPumpPatent-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewerSiphonPumpPatent_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NewerSiphonPumpPatent-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ram_pump" title="Ram pump">
        Ram pumps
       </a>
       do not work in this way. These metering pumps are true siphon pumping devices which use siphons as their power source.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inverted_siphon">
        Inverted siphon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inverted siphon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1677" data-file-width="2118" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg/220px-Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg/330px-Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg/440px-Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annotated_plumbing_trap.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Water seal under a sink. Inverted siphoning occurs below the line "A".
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Trap_(plumbing)" title="Trap (plumbing)">
        Trap (plumbing)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An
       <i>
        inverted siphon
       </i>
       is not a siphon but a term applied to pipes that must dip below an obstruction to form a "U" shaped flow path.
      </p>
      <p>
       Large inverted siphons are used to convey water being carried in
       <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">
        canals
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Flume" title="Flume">
        flumes
       </a>
       across valleys, for irrigation or gold mining. The Romans used inverted siphons of multiple lead pipes to cross valleys that were too big to construct an
       <a href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(bridge)" title="Aqueduct (bridge)">
        aqueduct
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Inverted siphons
       </i>
       are commonly called traps for their function in preventing smelly
       <a href="/wiki/Sewer_gas" title="Sewer gas">
        sewer gases
       </a>
       from coming back out of drains
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and sometimes making dense objects like rings and electronic components retrievable after falling into a drain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Liquid flowing in one end simply forces liquid up and out the other end, but solids like sand will accumulate. This is especially important in
       <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">
        sewage
       </a>
       systems or
       <a href="/wiki/Culvert" title="Culvert">
        culverts
       </a>
       which must be routed under rivers or other deep obstructions where the better term is "depressed sewer".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Back_siphonage">
        Back siphonage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Back siphonage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Back siphonage
       </i>
       is a plumbing term applied to the reversal of normal water flow in a plumbing system due to sharply reduced or negative pressure on the
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">
        water supply
       </a>
       side, such as high demand on water supply by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire-fighting" title="Fire-fighting">
        fire-fighting
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is not an actual siphon as it is
       <a href="/wiki/Suction" title="Suction">
        suction
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Back siphonage is rare as it depends on submerged inlets at the outlet (home) end and these are uncommon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water.epa.gov_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-water.epa.gov-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Back siphonage is not to be confused with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Backflow_(plumbing)" title="Backflow (plumbing)">
        backflow
       </a>
       ; which is the reversed flow of water from the outlet end to the supply end caused by pressure occurring at the outlet end.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water.epa.gov_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-water.epa.gov-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-siphon_valve">
        Anti-siphon valve
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anti-siphon valve">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Building_code" title="Building code">
        Building codes
       </a>
       often contain specific sections on back siphonage and especially for external
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faucet" title="Faucet">
        faucets
       </a>
       (See the sample building code quote, below).
       <a href="/wiki/Backflow_prevention_device" title="Backflow prevention device">
        Backflow prevention devices
       </a>
       such as
       <i>
        anti-siphon valves
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are required in such designs. The reason is that external faucets may be attached to hoses which may be immersed in an external body of water, such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Garden_pond" title="Garden pond">
        garden pond
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Swimming_pool" title="Swimming pool">
        swimming pool
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aquarium" title="Aquarium">
        aquarium
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Washing_machine" title="Washing machine">
        washing machine
       </a>
       . In these situations the flow is not actually a siphon but suction due to reduced pressure on the water supply side. Should the pressure within the water supply system fall, the external water may be returned by back pressure into the drinking water system through the faucet. Another possible contamination point is the water intake in the toilet tank. An anti-siphon valve is also required here to prevent pressure drops in the water supply line from suctioning water out of the toilet tank (which may contain additives such as "toilet blue"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and contaminating the water system. Anti-siphon valves function as a one-direction
       <a href="/wiki/Check_valve" title="Check valve">
        check valve
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Anti-siphon valves are also used medically.
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">
        Hydrocephalus
       </a>
       , or excess fluid in the brain, may be treated with a
       <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(medical)" title="Shunt (medical)">
        shunt
       </a>
       which drains
       <a href="/wiki/Cerebrospinal_fluid" title="Cerebrospinal fluid">
        cerebrospinal fluid
       </a>
       from the brain. All shunts have a valve to relieve excess pressure in the brain.
