The shunt may lead into the abdominal cavity such that the shunt outlet is significantly lower than the shunt intake when the patient is standing. Thus a siphon effect may take place and instead of simply relieving excess pressure, the shunt may act as a siphon, completely draining cerebrospinal fluid from the brain. The valve in the shunt may be designed to prevent this siphon action so that negative pressure on the drain of the shunt does not result in excess drainage. Only excess positive pressure from within the brain should result in drainage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Note that the anti-siphon valve in medical shunts is preventing excess forward flow of liquid. In plumbing systems, the anti-siphon valve is preventing backflow.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Sample building code regulations regarding "back siphonage" from the
        <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">
         Canadian province
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">
         Ontario
        </a>
       </i>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        7.6.2.3.Back Siphonage
        <ol>
         <li>
          Every potable water system that supplies a fixture or tank that is not subject to pressures above atmospheric shall be protected against back-siphonage by a
          <a href="/wiki/Backflow_prevention_device" title="Backflow prevention device">
           backflow preventer
          </a>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          Where a potable water supply is connected to a boiler, tank, cooling jacket, lawn sprinkler system or other device where a non-potable fluid may be under pressure that is above atmospheric or the water outlet may be submerged in the non-potable fluid, the water supply shall be protected against backflow by a backflow preventer.
         </li>
         <li>
          Where a hose bibb is installed outside a building, inside a garage, or where there is an identifiable risk of contamination, the potable water system shall be protected against backflow by a backflow preventer.
         </li>
        </ol>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_anti-siphoning_devices">
        Other anti-siphoning devices
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other anti-siphoning devices">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Along with anti-siphon valves,
       <i>
        anti-siphoning devices
       </i>
       also exist. The two are unrelated in application. Siphoning can be used to remove fuel from tanks. With the cost of fuel increasing, it has been linked in several countries to the rise in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_theft" title="Fuel theft">
        fuel theft
       </a>
       . Trucks, with their large fuel tanks, are most vulnerable. The anti-siphon device prevents thieves from inserting a tube into the fuel tank.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_barometer">
        Siphon barometer
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Siphon barometer">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        siphon barometer
       </i>
       is the term sometimes applied to the simplest of mercury
       <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">
        barometers
       </a>
       . A continuous U-shaped tube of the same diameter throughout is sealed on one end and filled with mercury. When placed into the upright, "U", position, mercury will flow away from the sealed end, forming a partial vacuum, until balanced by atmospheric pressure on the other end. The term "siphon" derives from the belief that air pressure is involved in the operation of a siphon. The difference in height of the fluid between the two arms of the U-shaped tube is the same as the maximum intermediate height of a siphon. When used to measure pressures other than atmospheric pressure, a siphon barometer is sometimes called a
       <i>
        siphon gauge
       </i>
       ; these are not siphons but follow a standard 'U'-shaped design
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       leading to the term. Siphon barometers are still produced as precision instruments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Siphon barometers should not be confused with a siphon rain gauge.,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_bottle">
        Siphon bottle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Siphon bottle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siphon_bottles.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="769" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Siphon_bottles.jpg/220px-Siphon_bottles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Siphon_bottles.jpg/330px-Siphon_bottles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Siphon_bottles.jpg/440px-Siphon_bottles.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siphon_bottles.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Siphon bottles
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        siphon bottle
       </i>
       (also called a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Soda_syphon" title="Soda syphon">
         soda syphon
        </a>
       </i>
       or, archaically, a
       <i>
        siphoid
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) is a pressurized bottle with a vent and a valve. It is not a siphon as pressure within the bottle drives the liquid up and out a tube. A special form was the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gasogene" title="Gasogene">
         gasogene
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphon_cup">
        Siphon cup
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Siphon cup">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <i>
        siphon cup
       </i>
       is the (hanging) reservoir of paint attached to a spray gun, it is not a siphon as a vacuum pump extracts the paint.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This name is to distinguish it from gravity-fed reservoirs. An archaic use of the term is a cup of oil in which the oil is transported out of the cup via a cotton wick or tube to a surface to be lubricated, this is not a siphon but an example of
       <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">
        capillary action
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Heron.27s_siphon">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Heron's_siphon">
        Heron's siphon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Heron's siphon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Heron's siphon
       </i>
       is not a siphon as it works as a gravity driven pressure pump,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at first glance it appears to be a
       <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_motion" title="Perpetual motion">
        perpetual motion
       </a>
       machine but will stop when the air in the priming pump is depleted. In a slightly differently configuration, it is also known as
       <a href="/wiki/Heron%27s_fountain" title="Heron's fountain">
        Heron's fountain
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Venturi_siphon">
        Venturi siphon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Venturi siphon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Venturi_effect" title="Venturi effect">
        venturi
       </a>
       siphon, also known as an
       <a href="/wiki/Aspirator_(pump)" title="Aspirator (pump)">
        eductor
       </a>
       , is not a siphon but a form of
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">
        vacuum pump
       </a>
       using the
       <a href="/wiki/Venturi_effect" title="Venturi effect">
        Venturi effect
       </a>
       of fast flowing
       <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">
        fluids
       </a>
       (e.g. air), to produce low pressures to
       <a href="/wiki/Suction" title="Suction">
        suction
       </a>
       other fluids; a common example is the
       <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">
        carburetor
       </a>
       . See
       <a href="/wiki/Pressure_head" title="Pressure head">
        pressure head
       </a>
       . The low pressure at the throat of the venturi is called a siphon when a second fluid is introduced, or an
       <a href="/wiki/Aspirator_(pump)" title="Aspirator (pump)">
        aspirator
       </a>
       when the fluid is air, this is an example of the misconception that air pressure is the operating force for siphons.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Siphonic_roof_drainage">
        Siphonic roof drainage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Siphonic roof drainage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Despite the name, siphonic roof drainage does not work as a siphon; the technology makes use of gravity induced vacuum pumping
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siphonic.com.au_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-siphonic.com.au-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to carry water horizontally from multiple roof drains to a single downpipe and to increase flow velocity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Metal baffles at the roof drain inlets reduce the injection of air which increases the efficiency of the system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-w3.unisa.edu.au_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-w3.unisa.edu.au-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One benefit to this drainage technique is reduced
       <a href="/wiki/Capital_cost" title="Capital cost">
        capital costs
       </a>
       in construction compared to traditional roof drainage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siphonic.com.au_68-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-siphonic.com.au-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another benefit is the elimination of pipe pitch or gradient required for conventional roof drainage piping. However this system of gravity pumping is mainly suitable for large buildings and is not usually suitable for residential properties.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-w3.unisa.edu.au_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-w3.unisa.edu.au-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Self-siphons">
        Self-siphons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Self-siphons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        self-siphon
       </i>
       is used in a number of ways. Liquids that are composed of long polymers can "self-siphon"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and these liquids do not depend on atmospheric pressure. Self-siphoning polymer liquids work the same as the siphon-chain model where the lower part of the chain pulls the rest of the chain up and over the crest. This phenomenon is also called a
       <i>
        tubeless siphon
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       "Self-siphon" is also often used in sales literature by siphon manufacturers to describe portable siphons that contain a pump. With the pump, no external suction (e.g. from a person's mouth/lungs) is required to start the siphon and thus the product is described as a "self-siphon".
      </p>
      <p>
       If the upper reservoir is such that the liquid there can rise above the height of the siphon crest, the rising liquid in the reservoir can "self-prime" the siphon and the whole apparatus be described as a "self-siphon".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Once primed, such a siphon will continue to operate until the level of the upper reservoir falls below the intake of the siphon. Such self-priming siphons are useful in some
       <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">
        rain gauges
       </a>
       and dams.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pascal%27s_siphon.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="894" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pascal%27s_siphon.png/220px-Pascal%27s_siphon.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pascal%27s_siphon.png/330px-Pascal%27s_siphon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pascal%27s_siphon.png/440px-Pascal%27s_siphon.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pascal%27s_siphon.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Pascal's siphon, showing two beakers of mercury inside a container of water, demonstrating that a siphon works by atmospheric pressure not that nature abhors a vacuum.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_nature">
        In nature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: In nature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">
        Anatomy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Anatomy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The term "siphon" is used for a number of structures in human and animal anatomy, either because flowing liquids are involved or because the structure is shaped like a siphon, but in which no actual siphon effect is occurring: see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Siphon_(disambiguation)" title="Siphon (disambiguation)">
        Siphon (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There has been a debate if whether the siphon mechanism plays a role in
       <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">
        blood
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">
        circulation
       </a>
       . However, in the 'closed loop' of circulation this was discounted; 'In contrast, in "closed" systems, like the circulation, gravity does not hinder uphill flow nor does it cause downhill flow, because gravity acts equally on the ascending and descending limbs of the circuit', but for 'historical reasons', the term is used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajpregu.physiology.org_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ajpregu.physiology.org-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One hypothesis (in 1989) was that a siphon existed in the circulation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">
        giraffe
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But further research, in 2004 found that, 'There is no hydrostatic gradient and since the "fall" of fluid does not assist the ascending arm, there is no siphon. The giraffe’s high arterial pressure, which is sufficient to raise the blood 2 m from heart to head with sufficient remaining pressure to perfuse the brain, supports this concept.'
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajpregu.physiology.org_76-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ajpregu.physiology.org-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, a paper written in 2005 urged more research on the hypothesis;
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        'The principle of the siphon is not species specific and should be a fundamental principle of closed circulatory systems. Therefore, the controversy surrounding the role of the siphon principle may best be resolved by a comparative approach. Analyses of blood pressure on a variety of long-necked and long-bodied animals, which take into account phylogenetic relatedness, will be important. In addition experimental studies that combined measurements of arterial and venous blood pressures, with cerebral blood flow, under a variety of gravitational stresses (different head positions), will ultimately resolve this controversy.'
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Species">
        Species
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Species">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some species are named after siphons because they resemble siphons in whole or in part.
       <a href="/wiki/Geosiphon" title="Geosiphon">
        Geosiphons
       </a>
       are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">
        fungi
       </a>
       . There are species of alga belonging to the
       <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
        family
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Siphonocladaceae" title="Siphonocladaceae">
        Siphonocladaceae
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">
        phylum
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">
        Chlorophyta
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which have tube-like structures.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ruellia_villosa" title="Ruellia villosa">
         Ruellia villosa
        </a>
       </i>
       is a tropical plant in the
       <a href="/wiki/Acanthaceae" title="Acanthaceae">
        Acanthaceae
       </a>
       family that is also known by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botanical_synonym" title="Botanical synonym">
        botanical synonym
       </a>
       , '
       <i>
        Siphonacanthus villosus
       </i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nees" title="Nees">
        Nees
       </a>
       '.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">
        Geology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Geology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Sump_(cave)" title="Sump (cave)">
        sump (cave)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In speleology, a siphon or a sump is that part of a cave passage that lies under water and through which cavers have to dive to progress further into the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_system" title="Cave system">
        cave system
       </a>
       , it is not an actual siphon.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kayaking">
        Kayaking
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Kayaking">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In canoe watersport "siphon" is called the life-dangerous situation that a part of the waterflow of a river flows underneath a stone or somewhat else and can dangerously suck in a paddler and his boat. Both can clog or stick to the entering orifice of that flow. Similar is a
       <a href="/wiki/Sieve" title="Sieve">
        sieve
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Explanation_using_Bernoulli.27s_equation">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Explanation_using_Bernoulli's_equation">
        Explanation using Bernoulli's equation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Explanation using Bernoulli's equation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_equation" title="Bernoulli's equation">
        Bernoulli's equation
       </a>
       may be applied to a siphon to derive the flow rate and maximum height of the siphon.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syphoning2.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="302" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Syphoning2.svg/220px-Syphoning2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Syphoning2.svg/330px-Syphoning2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Syphoning2.svg/440px-Syphoning2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syphoning2.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Example of a siphon with annotations to describe
         <a href="#Explanation_using_Bernoulli's_equation">
          Bernoulli's equation
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Let the surface of the upper reservoir be the reference elevation.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Let point A be the start point of siphon, immersed within the higher reservoir and at a depth −
        <i>
         d
        </i>
        below the surface of the upper reservoir.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Let point B be the intermediate high point on the siphon tube at height +
        <i>
         h
        </i>
        <sub>
         B
        </sub>
        above the surface of the upper reservoir.
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Let point C be the drain point of the siphon at height −
        <i>
         h
        </i>
        <sub>
         C
        </sub>
        below the surface of the upper reservoir.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Bernoulli's equation:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {v^{2} \over 2}+gy+{P \over \rho }=\mathrm {constant} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  v
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mi>
               y
              </mi>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mi>
                 P
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 ρ
                 <!-- ρ -->
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                c
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                o
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                n
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                s
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                t
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                a
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                n
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                t
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {v^{2} \over 2}+gy+{P \over \rho }=\mathrm {constant} }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" {v^2 \over 2}+gy+{P \over \rho}=\mathrm{constant} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/88da84c4e4c91a42522611e28bcd6bb51a0211be" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:25.318ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
       <dd>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Velocity">
        Velocity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>

         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As the siphon is a single system, the constant in all four equations is the same. Setting equations 1 and 4 equal to each other gives:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>

           <semantics>

                </mn>

               +
              </mo>

              </mi>

               (
              </mo>

              </mn>

               <mfrac>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  P
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">
                    a
                   </mi>
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">

                </mi>

                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {0^{2} \over 2}+g(0)+{P_{\mathrm {atm} } \over \rho }={v_{C}^{2} \over 2}-gh_{C}+{P_{\mathrm {atm} } \over \rho }}

          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{0^2 \over 2}+g(0)+{P_\mathrm{atm} \over \rho}={v_C^2 \over 2}-gh_C+{P_\mathrm{atm} \over \rho}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/78c5b6c8c65fd12367aee9880d93288f1beb0079" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:39.764ex; height:6.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Solving for
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Velocity of siphon:
        </b>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{C}={\sqrt {2gh_{C}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                v
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 C
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <msqrt>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
                <mi>
                 g
                </mi>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  h
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi>
                   C
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </msqrt>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle v_{C}={\sqrt {2gh_{C}}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="v_C=\sqrt{2gh_C}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/13ca97134122ff268ead3f4c0c24d101e50f4183" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:13.13ex; height:3.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The velocity of the siphon is thus driven solely by the height difference between the surface of the upper reservoir and the drain point. The height of the intermediate high point,
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       , does not affect the velocity of the siphon. However, as the siphon is a single system,
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       =
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
       and the intermediate high point does limit the maximum velocity. The drain point cannot be lowered indefinitely to increase the velocity. Equation 3 will limit the velocity to a positive pressure at the intermediate high point to prevent
       <a href="/wiki/Cavitation" title="Cavitation">
        cavitation
       </a>
       . The maximum velocity may be calculated by combining equations 1 and 3:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {0^{2} \over 2}+g(0)+{P_{\mathrm {atm} } \over \rho }={v_{B}^{2} \over 2}+gh_{B}+{P_{B} \over \rho }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>

                 <mn>
                  0
                 </mn>

                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mn>
               0
              </mn>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  P
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">

                   </mi>
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">

                 </mrow>

                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>

              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <msubsup>
                 <mi>

                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                 </mrow>

                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>

              </mo>
              <mi>

              </mi>

               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                </mi>

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{0^2 \over 2}+g(0)+{P_\mathrm{atm} \over \rho}={v_B^2 \over 2}+gh_B+{P_B \over \rho}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/851e8c5c3db9b79cbb4f2d2f6a59678f48c0bb63" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:38.178ex; height:6.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Setting
       <i>
        P
       </i>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       = 0 and solving for
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        max
       </sub>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Maximum velocity of siphon:
        </b>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{\mathrm {max} }={\sqrt {2\left({P_{\mathrm {atm} } \over \rho }-gh_{B}\right)}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

                 </mi>

              </mo>

                </mn>

                 </mo>

                  </mo>

                  </mi>

                    </mi>

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="v_\mathrm{max}=\sqrt{2\left({P_\mathrm{atm} \over \rho}-gh_B\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bf06f5f3d3373dc937e956d69f0d9bd4f17e2219" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:26.978ex; height:7.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The depth, −
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       , of the initial entry point of the siphon in the upper reservoir, does not affect the velocity of the siphon. No limit to the depth of the siphon start point is implied by Equation 2 as pressure
       <i>
        P
       </i>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       increases with depth
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       . Both these facts imply the operator of the siphon may bottom skim or top skim the upper reservoir without impacting the siphon's performance.
      </p>
      <p>
       Note that this equation for the velocity is the same as that of any object falling height
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
       . Note also that this equation assumes
       <i>
        P
       </i>
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
       is atmospheric pressure. If the end of the siphon is below the surface, the height to the end of the siphon cannot be used; rather the height difference between the reservoirs should be used.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Maximum_height">
        Maximum height
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Maximum height">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Although siphons can exceed the barometric height of the liquid in special circumstances, e.g. when the liquid is degassed and the tube is clean and smooth,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in general the practical maximum height can be found as follows.
      </p>
      <p>
       Setting equations 1 and 3 equal to each other gives:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

              </mo>

              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                <msub>

                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

      <p>
       Maximum height of the intermediate high point occurs when it is so high that the pressure at the intermediate high point is zero; in typical scenarios this will cause the liquid to form bubbles and if the bubbles enlarge to fill the pipe then the siphon will "break". Setting
       <i>

       </sub>
       = 0:

       Solving for

      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>

        </b>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">

      <p>
       This means that the height of the intermediate high point is limited by pressure along the streamline being always greater than zero.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Maximum height of siphon:
        </b>
       </dd>
       <dd>

                 </mrow>
                </msub>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  ρ
                  <!-- ρ -->
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  g
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle h_{B\mathrm {,max} }={P_{\mathrm {atm} } \over \rho g}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="h_{B\mathrm{,max}}={P_\mathrm{atm} \over \rho g}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2604f48bd906c546720b433a6329caded4d90399" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:14.824ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This is the maximum height that a siphon will work. Substituting values will give approximately 10 metres for water and, by definition of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_pressure" title="Standard pressure">
        standard pressure
       </a>
       , 0.76 metres (760 mm or 30 in) for mercury. The ratio of heights (about 13.6) equals the ratio of densities of water and mercury (at a given temperature). Note that as long as this condition is satisfied (pressure greater than zero), the flow at the output of the siphon is still only governed by the height difference between the source surface and the outlet. Volume of fluid in the apparatus is not relevant as long as the pressure head remains above zero in every section. Because pressure drops when velocity is increased, a static siphon (or manometer) can have a slightly higher height than a flowing siphon.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vacuum_siphons">
        Vacuum siphons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Vacuum siphons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg">
          <img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
         </a>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          <b>
           This section needs expansion
          </b>
          with: explanation of theory of operation.
          <small>
           You can help by
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">
            adding to it
           </a>
           .
          </small>
          <small>
           <i>
            (December 2012)
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Experiments have shown that siphons can operate in a vacuum, via
       <a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(chemistry)" title="Cohesion (chemistry)">
        cohesion
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">
        tensile strength
       </a>
       between molecules, provided that the liquids are pure and degassed and surfaces are very clean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minor1914_5-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Minor1914-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boatwright2011_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Boatwright2011-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duane1902_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Duane1902-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nokes_8-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nokes-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramette2010_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ramette2010-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxford_English_Dictionary">
        <i>
         Oxford English Dictionary
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siphon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Oxford English Dictionary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
         Oxford English Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       (OED) entry on
       <i>
        siphon
       </i>
       , published in 1911, states that a siphon works by
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">
        atmospheric pressure
       </a>
       . Stephen Hughes of
       <a href="/wiki/Queensland_University_of_Technology" title="Queensland University of Technology">
        Queensland University of Technology
       </a>
       criticized this in a 2010 article
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hughes-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which was widely reported in the media.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The OED editors stated, "there is continuing debate among scientists as to which view is correct. ... We would expect to reflect this debate in the fully updated entry for siphon, due to be published later this year."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dr. Hughes continued to defend his view of the siphon in a late September post at the Oxford blog.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 2015 definition by the OED is:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        A tube used to convey liquid upwards from a reservoir and then down to a lower level of its own accord. Once the liquid has been forced into the tube, typically by suction or immersion, flow continues unaided.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">
        Encyclopædia Britannica
       </a>
       currently describes a siphon as:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        Siphon, also spelled syphon, instrument, usually in the form of a tube bent to form two legs of unequal length, for conveying liquid over the edge of a vessel and delivering it at a lower level. Siphons may be of any size. The action depends upon the influence of gravity (not, as sometimes thought, on the difference in atmospheric pressure; a siphon will work in a vacuum) and upon the cohesive forces that prevent the columns of liquid in the legs of the siphon from breaking under their own weight. At sea level, water can be lifted a little more than 10 metres (33 feet) by a siphon. In civil engineering, pipelines called inverted siphons are used to carry sewage or stormwater under streams, highway cuts, or other depressions in the ground. In an inverted siphon the liquid completely fills the pipe and flows under pressure, as opposed to the open-channel gravity flow that occurs in most sanitary or storm sewers.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
         <a href="#cite_note-94">
          [94]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
